Better to derive necessary conditions. Let's denote the set of points P_n. The lines we use are distinct. For each line, consider its "type" (horizontal, vertical, slope -1, or other). The condition that exactly k lines are sunny means that the other n-k lines are from the three "bad" families. We need to cover all points. This is reminiscent of a covering problem where we have a triangular grid and we want to cover it with lines. There is a known result: The maximum number of points that can be covered by a union of rows, columns, and sums is something like? Actually we can think of the complement: The points that are not on any of the chosen rows, columns, or sums are those that are "far" from the axes. The number of such points is something like (n - a)(n - b)/2? Not sure.

We can try to derive a necessary condition on k using an invariant. Consider the sum over all points of something like (-1)^{a+b} or something. For a line that is not parallel to the three bad directions, the points on it have a certain pattern. Perhaps we can use a double counting of the number of points on lines of each type.

Another idea: Consider the set of points. For each point (a,b), define its "weight" w(a,b) = 1. The total sum is T_n. Now, consider the sum over all lines of the number of points on that line. This sum is at least T_n. For a non-sunny line, it contains at most n points. For a sunny line, it contains at most something like? But we can bound the number of points on a sunny line by a function of n. However, we need a lower bound on the number of points that can be covered by sunny lines. Perhaps we can use the fact that the points are in a triangular grid, and the non-sunny lines are from three families. If we have many non-sunny lines, they cover many points, but the complement points are "sparse". In particular, if we have n-k non-sunny lines, the complement size is at least something like? There is a known result: For a triangular grid, the maximum number of points that can be covered by a union of rows, columns, and sums is at most something like? Actually we can use a combinatorial argument: The points are (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. Consider the set of points with a+b = constant. There are n+1 such sums. The non-sunny lines of slope -1 cover entire sums. Horizontal lines cover rows. Vertical lines cover columns. So if we have a set of horizontal lines, they cover certain rows. If we have a set of vertical lines, they cover certain columns. If we have a set of slope -1 lines, they cover certain sums. The complement points are those that are not in any of these chosen rows, columns, or sums. This is a classic problem: The minimum number of points that can be left uncovered by a union of k rows, columns, and sums is something like? There is a known result: For a triangular grid, the maximum number of points that can be covered by a union of r rows, c columns, and s sums is at most something like? But we need to show that with n-2 lines (non-sunny), the complement size is at least 3, and that those points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. However, we already found a counterexample for n=6: with 4 non-sunny lines (y=1,y=2,y=3,x+y=7), the complement size is 1 point, which is less than 3. So that argument fails. So for n=6, k=2 is possible because we can choose non-sunny lines that leave only 1 point uncovered. So the complement size can be 1. So the necessary condition is not about the complement size being at least 3. Instead, we need to see if the uncovered points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. For n=6, we had 1 uncovered point, which can be covered by 1 sunny line. So that works. So for k=2, it's possible if we can choose n-2 non-sunny lines that leave at most 2 points uncovered, and those points can be covered by 2 sunny lines (each line covering at most 2 points, but if there are 2 points, they can be on a sunny line, so one sunny line suffices). Actually if we have 2 points uncovered, we can cover them with one sunny line if they are collinear on a sunny line, or with two sunny lines if they are not. So we need to see if we can achieve a complement of size 2 or 1 with n-2 non-sunny lines. For n=6, we achieved complement 1. For n=7, can we achieve complement 1 or 2 with 5 non-sunny lines? Possibly. So k=2 might be possible for n≥6. For n=4, complement size with 2 lines is at least 3; so k=2 impossible. For n=5, complement size with 3 lines is at least 3; so k=2 impossible. So the threshold is n≥6. So for n=6, k=2 works; for n=7, we need to test.
